1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""

The "absorb" trick (like FlashMLA/DeepSeek MLA): instead of materializing per-token k_nope = kv_b_k @ c_kv, you can absorb kv_b into the query: q_nope_absorbed = q_nope @ kv_b_k (per head: [1,128] @ [128... wait. scores[h,l] = q_nope[h] · k_nope[l,h] + q_rope[h] · k_rope[l] k_nope[l,h] = c_kv[l] @ W_uk[h] where W_uk[h] is [512, 128] slice of kv_b. So q_nope[h] · k_nope[l,h] = q_nope[h] @ (c_kv[l] @ W_uk[h])^T... = (q_nope[h] @ W_uk[h]^T) @ c_kv[l]. Define q_latent[h] = W_uk[h] @ q_nope[h] ∈ R^512. Then score contribution = q_latent[h] · c_kv[l]. Similarly, o[h] = sum_l p[l,h] v[l,h], v[l,h] = W_uv[h] @ c_kv[l]... wait v[l,h,d] = sum_c c_kv[l,c] W_uv[h,c,d] = (c_kv[l] @ W_uv[h])[d]. o[h] = sum_l p[l,h] (c_kv[l] @ W_uv[h]) = (sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l]) @ W_uv[h]. So define o_latent[h] = sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l] ∈ R^512, then o[h] = o_latent[h] @ W_uv[h]. This is the classic MLA absorb: attention is done entirely in the 512-d latent space, avoiding materializing [ctx, 32, 256] dequantized values. Strategy considerations: - Batch-1 decode is memory-bound. The kernel must stream ~260MB of weights/cache. Single kernel with a grid sized to fill the GPU (RTX PRO 6000 has... let me check: it's Blackwell SM120, 188 SMs? The RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell has 188 SMs? Let me check with nvidia-smi.) - The challenge is sequencing: layer n+1 needs layer n's output. Within one kernel launch, we need global synchronization across thread blocks between layers. Options: 1. **Cooperative grid sync** (cooperative groups in CUDA, `cooperative_groups::this_grid().sync()`) — requires launching with `cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel`, grid must fit in one wave. Triton doesn't support grid sync. 2. **Software barriers**: atomic counter + spin-wait. Works with any launch, but requires all blocks resident (grid ≤ max co-resident), otherwise deadlock. We can size the grid to the number of SMs × blocks-per-SM, guaranteed resident if occupancy allows. 3. **Single wave persistent kernel**: grid = number of SMs (or 2×SMs), each block does slices of work across all layers, with software global barriers between stages. This is the standard "megakernel decode" approach. Since the whole thing is memory-bound and we need ~260MB streamed, we want maximum bandwidth: need enough blocks/threads in flight. One block per SM might not saturate BW; typically want several blocks per SM or many warps. With a persistent kernel we can choose grid = 188 SMs × 2 or 4 blocks. Software barrier between phases: classic pattern (arrive-and-wait with atomicAdd on a counter plus generation flag). Deadlock-safe only if all blocks are resident simultaneously. We can guarantee that via cooperative launch (CUDA) or by carefully computing occupancy (Triton). With Triton, blocks are not guaranteed co-resident, but in practice with grid ≤ SM count × min blocks per SM and each block using minimal resources, they will be. Risky but standard. Actually people do "spin-lock global barrier" in Triton persistent kernels a lot (e.g., in some production kernels). Hmm. Alternative: CUDA C++ with load_inline + cooperative groups grid sync. That's guaranteed safe. Writing a full CUDA megakernel with int4 unpack, bf16 math... bf16 conversions in CUDA are easy (`__nv_bfloat16`). Vectorized loads (uint4 = 32 int4 values). Grid sync needs cooperative launch — supported via load_inline? We can write our own launch using cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel in the C++ wrapper. Yes. But writing the entire thing in raw CUDA with software pipelining is a lot of code. Triton is much more productive. But Triton has no grid sync... but we can do atomics-based software barrier in Triton: `tl.atomic_add` on a counter, then spin on `tl.load(counter, volatile=True)`. People have done this (barrier via atomics in Triton megakernels, e.g., "persistent matmul with grid sync"). There is a risk: without cooperative launch, blocks may not all be resident. If grid size ≤ min occupancy × SMs, in practice all blocks are resident because the GPU schedules blocks greedily. It's technically UB but universally works if resources fit. Many production Triton megakernels (e.g. some MoE kernels?) do this. Triton pros: fast to write tl.dot, softmax, reductions; good codegen. Cons: software barrier is hand-rolled (fine); per-block phase dispatch = one big kernel with `for phase in range(N)` and if/else on pid (Triton handles multi-phase via sequential code with barriers in between — natural); launch overhead ~5-10µs (must optimize via cached launcher); can't easily do cp.async / TMA subtleties (Triton does auto async copies on Ampere+). CUDA pros: full control (cooperative grid.sync() guaranteed deadlock-free; launch via cooperative API ~3µs; __pipeline async copy; mma.sync for attention if needed — but writing bf16 mma by hand is a lot). Cons: development time, softmax/reductions manual. Actually — key insight about phases: we have ~26 sequential phases with very different parallelism structure. Triton: one kernel, each phase loops over its work items with `idx = pid*X ...`; barriers between. But careful: Triton kernel must have ALL phases' code in one function with `tl.while`/for — it's just straight-line code with for loops; the barrier function called between. The barrier in Triton: ``` @triton.jit def gbar(bar_ptr, gen_ptr, phase_count, G: tl.constexpr): # arrive tl.debug_barrier() # intra-block? no that's CTA sync if thread0: atomic_add spin... ``` In Triton, all code is per-program (block-level); atomic ops are per-element ops on tensors. A scalar atomic: `tl.atomic_add(bar_ptr, 1)` returns old. Spin: `while tl.atomic_add(bar_ptr+1, 0) < target: pass` — careful, atomic_add with 0 generates traffic; use `tl.load(bar_ptr+1, volatile=True)`? Triton has tl.load(ptr, cache_modifier...) — volatile supported? There's `tl.load(ptr, volatile=True)`?? In older versions there was a volatile argument... Triton 3.x: tl.load doesn't expose volatile directly; but `tl.atomic_add(ptr, 0)` works and bypasses L1 (atomics go to L2). Spinning with atomics is fine-ish (contends at L2 but it's a short window). Design the barrier as two counters: arrival counter per generation. Standard: ``` gen = tl.load(gen_ptr) # current generation, or keep in register old = tl.atomic_add(arrive_ptr, 1) if old == last: store target = ... ``` Simpler robust: sense-reversal with two counters... In a persistent kernel with sequential phases, use per-phase barrier slots: bar[i] counter; each block atomic_add(bar[i],1); then spin while load(bar[i]) < G. Since all blocks pass phases in the same order and bar[i] is only re-used... never re-used (26 slots, allocated per launch — pass a workspace zero'ed... who zeroes? The kernel itself can't zero before all blocks read... We can allocate a small workspace via torch.zeros ONCE at init (persistent kernel launched MANY times — but each launch needs fresh zeros!). Zeroing workspace per launch = extra kernel. Alternative: monotonically increasing targets: never reset; targets = G, 2G, 3G... accumulate across launches?? Fragile across trials/models. OR: zero the barrier array at the END of the kernel (last phase: block 0 resets all 26 counters to 0, then a final barrier? no...). Hmm: the reset must complete before ANY block of the NEXT launch arrives. Next launch same stream → starts after this launch completes (same CUDA stream, serialized). So block-0-resets-at-end is safe (kernel completion itself orders it... wait — other blocks might still be running when block 0 resets counter i? Block0 resets after the FINAL barrier; all blocks passed final barrier = all blocks are past the last use of ALL barrier slots?? The final barrier ensures everyone arrived at the end; after that, blocks exit (no more barrier use). Block 0 resets after final barrier — but others might EXIT the final barrier while block0 hasn't reset... they won't touch barriers again this launch. Next launch sees zeroed — but only after this kernel fully completes (serialization). Safe! Except: if kernel errors/aborts... fine. Alternative classic: sense-reversal single counter, target = G: arrive: old=atomic_add(cnt,1); if old==G-1 { st.release cnt=0 (reset!) then st flag=gen+1 } else spin while flag==gen. Simpler with monotonic flag. Let me use: two-slot scheme with generation counter in shared... Actually simplest correct: ``` gen_ptr (flag), cnt_ptr (counter) g = load(flag) [each block reads once per barrier — but all blocks' history consistent] t = atomic_add(cnt, 1) if t == G-1: store cnt=0; atomic_add(flag,1) (release semantics - need fences) else: while load(flag)==g: pass (acquire) ``` Memory model in Triton: atomics are relaxed by default but Triton generates atom with sem flags? tl.atomic_add supports `sem` argument ('acquire','release','acq_rel','relaxed'). We need release on arrival (so prior writes visible) and acquire on flag read. Triton atomics default to acq_rel? Default sem is acq_rel I believe. GPU scope: need `.gpu` scope (Triton default scope for atomic ops is gpu). Plus the data written (e.g., o buffer) must be visible to other blocks' loads after barrier: release-write by last arriver + acquire-reads by waiters + the atomic RMWs themselves chain it. However blocks' plain loads may hit stale L1! On sm_80+, L1 is per-SM and NOT coherent; loads bypass L1 only with .cg modifier. If block A wrote X to global (through its L1? no — stores are write-through to L2 on NVIDIA L1; stores go to L2) and block B loads X with default .ca load, B might hit stale L1 line IF B had previously loaded that address. So loads of cross-block data must use cache_modifier='.cg' (L2 only) or volatile. Triton tl.load(cache_modifier='.cg') exists. Writes: default .wb writes are fine (L2-coherent at write). To be safe: all loads consuming data produced by other blocks use cache_modifier... or the barrier arrival includes `threadfence` semantics making... no — fence orders MY ops; consumer needs to not read stale L1: use .cg or ensure consumer never touched that address before (true for first touch but buffers are reused across phases!). Hmm wait, but within a phase, does data go through L2 anyway? GPU L1 on modern archs is write-through, so writes are visible in L2 immediately-ish; reader with .ca who never read the line before gets it from... L1 miss → L2 → correct data. Staleness only if reader previously loaded the same line into ITS L1 and it's still cached. Buffers reused across phases (e.g., x_norm buffer read by GEMV blocks in phase i and phase i+3 by different blocks) — could be stale in some block's L1 if that block read it in a previous phase. So: use cache_modifier='.cg' (or volatile) on ALL loads of cross-phase buffers + phase-produced data. Costs small (L2 BW is high). Also stores: use default (or .cg) — Triton stores are .wb → write-back L2, fine; some prefer .cs (streaming) for big buffers. Similarly weight loads: read-only, safe with .ca, but big streaming loads better with '.cg' or even eviction hints. Fine-tune later. Triton also inserts its own `barrier` (CTA-level) for smem reuse — that's tl.debug_barrier() only if called. Within a block, Triton manages smem consistency automatically across tl.load/dot sequences. OK. Now, per-block phase work distribution: G blocks fixed grid. For each phase, work items = column-chunks of each GEMV (for KDA qkvg: 4 proj × 4096 cols / BLOCK_N each...). Program id p loops over assigned items: `for it in range(p, num_items, G)`: compute item. This static striding is standard persistent style. Items across phases must all complete before next phase — barrier does that. class CompiledKernel:

    def __init__(self, src, metadata_group, hash):
        from collections import namedtuple
        metadata_path = next((Path(p) for c, p in metadata_group.items() if c.endswith(".json")))
        metadata = json.loads(metadata_path.read_text())
        # JSON serialization dumps the target as a dict. Restore it to a GPUTarget.
        target = metadata['target']
        metadata['target'] = GPUTarget(target['backend'], target['arch'], target['warp_size'])
        KernelMetadata = namedtuple('KernelMetadata', sorted(list(metadata.keys())))
        self.metadata = KernelMetadata(**metadata)
        backend = make_backend(self.metadata.target)

Hmm, actually: "Cooperative kernels require exclusive access to the GPU" — no wait, they don't require exclusivity; grid.sync requires co-residency of ITS OWN grid. The driver MAY prevent concurrent execution of cooperative kernels with other cooperative kernels. Actually the real statement: for grid.sync() correctness, all thread blocks of the grid must be resident simultaneously. CUDA guarantees this for cooperative launches by validating the grid size against device occupancy — but this guarantee assumes the whole device is available to the context. If another process's blocks are running, the CUDA scheduler timeslices: cooperative kernels do get scheduled correctly — the runtime serializes them against other work. In practice, a cooperative launch while another kernel is running → our launch blocks (device-side) until the other kernel finishes, then our whole grid runs. That's what we want! No deadlock, guaranteed correctness. And Triton exposes launch_cooperative_grid (it's a compile option: `kernel[grid](..., launch_cooperative_grid=True)`. Let me verify it works with spin barriers — with cooperative launch, ALL my blocks are resident at once (that's the guarantee), so my software barrier is then formally sound. **Option B: make barriers tolerate arbitrary scheduling (like counting on forward progress)**: impossible generally. **Option C: avoid grid-wide barriers**: restructure as dataflow kernel where blocks... no, sequential layer dependencies need syncs. So: use `launch_cooperative_grid=True`. Concerns: - The driver limits cooperative grid size to max co-resident blocks (queried via occupancy API); with 188 blocks × 512 threads ≤ 1 block/SM guaranteed fits. - If ANOTHER process's kernel is RUNNING when we launch: our launch... with cooperative launch, CUDA uses "stream serialization": our kernel starts only when resources allow ALL blocks resident — with another context's kernel running indefinitely, we'd wait — not deadlock in their case (their kernels end eventually). Bench noise at worst. OK. - Deadlock between TWO cooperative kernels from two processes? CUDA driver co-schedules; timeslicing resolves. So each block: 18 × (load latency ~800ns + compute) ≈ 18×1µs ≈ 18-25µs. THAT MATCHES 24.5µs! The whole phase is latency-bound per block. Fix: SOFTWARE PIPELINING: prefetch next iteration's tile while computing current. In Triton: manual prefetch: ``` wq_next = load(tile k=0) for r in range(0, KCH, BK): wq = wq_next if r + BK < KCH: wq_next = load(tile r+BK) ... compute with wq ... ``` Triton schedules async? Regular tl.load is a blocking ld.global → the compiler moves the load's results use point... ld.global is async in the sense that the scoreboard stalls at USE. Issuing load for iter+1 before compute of iter i allows overlap: the ld is issued (nonblocking), then compute on current tile, the use of prefetched tile stalls only next iteration. This is the classic pattern. Also s0/z0/x prefetch similarly. 2. The strided x loads (2*arange stride 32b): fine. 3. Also should use `tl.max_contiguous` hints? Blocks must all agree. Keep `tgt` as Python int? NO — plain Python ints in Triton AST get baked as constants... A Triton kernel is traced once: Python locals of type int ARE constants UNLESS assigned tensor values. But `tgt += 1` in traced straight-line code: the AST rewriter handles it as constexpr arithmetic — since STATIC here (no runtime flow), it stays a compile-time constant chain. Since the whole kernel body is compile-time unrolled (static_range loops), literal barrier ordinals get baked ✓ — SAME as hardcoding slot indices but cleaner. Actually YES: target values are compile-time; the check `while g < target` compares runtime gen vs constexpr target ✓ perfect. So the flow with myt being a Python int in the jit body: ``` tgt = 0 ... phase ... gbar(bar_ptr, tgt + 1, G); tgt += 1 ``` The AST transformer handles tgt as constexpr int (assignments of Python expressions). Shared helper: dequant-GEMV column-chunk over given x-source: ```python @triton.jit def dq_tile(w_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, x_ptr_or_smem?, ...) ``` Since x comes from DIFFERENT sources per phase (x0 bf16, ACCUM fp32, H bf16 via norm, OBUF/QBUF/etc.), write GEMV item bodies inline per phase but via a common helper that takes x as a GLOBAL pointer + needed transform flags? The norm-application variants: - KDA P1 / MLA P1: xn = rmsnorm(x, anorm_w): rrms computed per item redundantly. - MoE P5: xn = rmsnorm(H, mnorm_w): rrms redundant per item. - down P6: x = INTERM (bf16, raw). - o_proj P3: x = OBUF (bf16 raw). - MLA P2 (q_lat): x = QBUF slice (bf16 raw). - MLA P4: x = o_lat fp32 freshly merged in-item (registers—different form (out[j] = Σ_c x[c] w[c,j] contract over in-dim K — standard). I'll write a parametrized helper with flags: NORM: 0 none, 1 = attn-norm (x from XPTR with dtype... just pass the *post-norm* logic: helper receives x_ptr bf16-or-fp32 flag + norm weight ptr flag: ```python @triton.jit def load_xn(X, AN, rrms, k0, BF16SRC: tl.constexpr, HASNORM: tl.constexpr, BV: tl.constexpr): offs = k0 + tl.arange(0, BV) if BF16SRC: x = tl.load(X + offs, cache_modifier=".cg").to(tl.float32) else: x = tl.load(X + offs, cache_modifier=".cg") if HASNORM: w = tl.load(AN + offs, cache_modifier=".cg").to(tl.float32) x = (x * rrms * w).to(tl.bfloat16).to(tl.float32) return x ``` rrms computed per item: ```python @triton.jit def comp_rrms(X, BF16SRC: tl.constexpr): offs = tl.arange(0, 4096) # mask < 2304 m = offs < 2304 if BF16SRC: x = tl.load(X+offs, mask=m, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) else: x = tl.load(X+offs, mask=m, other=0.0) return tl.rsqrt(tl.sum(x*x) / 2304 + 1e-6) ``` Matches torch .mean(-1) = sum/2304 + eps INSIDE rsqrt ✓. +is one persistent Triton kernel launched exactly once per step().
+
+Grid: 188 programs (one per SM on RTX PRO 6000), cooperative launch so all
+blocks are co-resident; cross-phase sequencing is a single-slot generation
+barrier (all blocks arrive exactly once per phase, last arriver bumps the
+generation counter).
+

+def fast_ref_model(cfg):
+    """reference.Model without the slow CPU re-quantization."""
+    rm = reference.Model.__new__(reference.Model)
+    nn.Module.__init__(rm)
+    rm.cfg = cfg
+    rm.blocks = nn.ModuleList(reference.Block(cfg, k) for k in cfg.pattern)

Prefetch-pipelined: ``` xns = _p_bf16(P, GP_XNS) + pid * 4096 acc2 = tl.zeros((64, BN), tl.float32) wq0 = tl.load(wb + (r0 + tl.arange(0, 64))[:, None] * wN + n0 + tl.arange(0, BN)[None, :], cache_modifier=".cg") for r in range(0, KCH, 64): rn = tl.minimum(r + 64, KCH - 64) wq = tl.load(wb + (r0 + rn + tl.arange(0, 64))[:, None] * wN + n0 + tl.arange(0, BN)[None, :], cache_modifier=".cg") g = (2 * (r0 + r)) // 128 s0 = tl.load(sb + g * wN + n0 + tl.arange(0, BN), cache_modifier=".cg").to(tl.float32) z0 = tl.load(zb + g * wN + n0 + tl.arange(0, BN), cache_modifier=".cg").to(tl.float32) xlo = tl.load(xns + r + tl.arange(0, 64)).to(tl.float32) xhi = tl.load(xns + 2048 + r + tl.arange(0, 64)).to(tl.float32) wlo = ((wq0 & 0xF).to(tl.float32) - z0[None, :]) * s0[None, :] whi = ((wq0 >> 4).to(tl.float32) - z0[None, :]) * s0[None, :] acc2 += xlo[:, None] * wlo + xhi[:, None] * whi wq0 = wq return tl.sum(acc2, 0) ``` Similar for [64] column variants (BN=64). reasoning
Wait, the beta computation got complicated with the interleaving. The beta_w layout is (32, 2304) with y[o] = Σ_i w[o,i] x[i]. With my xlo/xhi split: x[2i]=xlo[i], x[2i+1]=xhi[i]. Load wt (32, 256) over all 2304/… let me simplify: iterate r over 1152 (K2) in chunks of 128 packed positions: wt tile [32, 256] at ilo = 2*(r..r+128) covers w[:, 2r:2r+256]. The product: wt[o, 2j] * xlo[r+j] + wt[o, 2j+1] * xhi[r+j]. Instead of the reshape gymnastics, load lo/hi weight columns separately... the weight tile columns are interleaved. My tl.sum over (1,2) requires shapes matching — reshape wt (32,128,2) and xe (128,2) then broadcast multiply sum over axes 1,2. tl.sum(x, (1,2)) — does Triton support multi-axis reduce? tl.sum with axis tuple is supported in recent versions. Simpler and safer: accb += tl.sum(wl * lo[None, :], 1) + tl.sum(wh * hi[None, :], 1) where wl = wt[:, 0::2]... slicing stride on loaded tile NOT allowed. Load wl and wh as separate loads instead: wl = load(bw + o*2304 + ilo) where ilo = 2*ar(128) → strided-2 bf16 loads (same as before but only once per item, ~9 iterations)! Fine — just do that (no fancy reshapes). Let me simplify.
assistant
